</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">Mimesis</a>
</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <a href="/wiki/Natural_slavery" title="Natural slavery">Natural slavery</a>
</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_virtue" title="Intellectual virtue">Intellectual virtues</a>: <a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)" title="Sophia (wisdom)">sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Episteme" title="Episteme">episteme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">nous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phronesis" title="Phronesis">phronesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Techne" title="Techne">techne</a>

</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr class="note"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influences</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phaleas_of_Chalcedon" title="Phaleas of Chalcedon">Phaleas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hippodamus_of_Miletus" title="Hippodamus of Miletus">Hippodamus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hippias" title="Hippias">Hippias</a></li></ul>
</div></div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr class="note"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">
<p>Virtually all subsequent <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_philosophy" title="Middle Eastern philosophy">Middle Eastern</a> philosophy, especially <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> including <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicennism" title="Avicennism">Avicennism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aristotelianism_(literature)" title="Neo-Aristotelianism (literature)">Literary Neo-Aristotelianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maimonideanism" title="Maimonideanism">Maimonideanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Objectivism" title="Objectivism">Objectivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peripatetics" title="Peripatetics">Peripatetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Llullism" title="Llullism">Llullism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-scholasticism" title="Neo-scholasticism">Neo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotism" title="Scotism">Scotism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_scholasticism" title="Second scholasticism">Second</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a>, etc), <a href="/wiki/School_of_Brentano" title="School of Brentano">School of Brentano</a>, additionally <a href="/wiki/Middle_Platonism" title="Middle Platonism">Middle Platonism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> as well as significant amounts of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> especially <a href="/wiki/Protoscience" title="Protoscience">protoscience</a>.
</p><p>See: <a href="/wiki/List_of_writers_influenced_by_Aristotle" title="List of writers influenced by Aristotle">List of writers influenced by Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_Aristotle" title="Commentaries on Aristotle">Commentaries on Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Aristotle" title="Pseudo-Aristotle">Pseudo-Aristotle</a>
</p>
</div></div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Aristotle</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/Ã¦r/: 'arr' in 'marry'">Ã¦r</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/É/: 'o' in 'body'">É</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/Él/: 'le' in 'bottle'">Él</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins_English_Dictionary_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins_English_Dictionary-5">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">á¼ÏÎ¹ÏÏÎ¿ÏÎ­Î»Î·Ï</span></span> <i>AristotÃ©lÄs</i>, <small>pronouncedÂ </small><span class="IPA" lang="el-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[aristotÃ©lÉËs]</a></span>; 384â322Â BC) was a Greek <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical period</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>. Taught by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, he was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a> of philosophy within the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(classical)" title="Lyceum (classical)">Lyceum</a> and the wider <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> tradition. His writings cover many subjects including <a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_geology" title="History of geology">geology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>. Aristotle provided a complex synthesis of the various philosophies existing prior to him. It was above all from his teachings that <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">the West</a> inherited its intellectual <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a>, as well as problems and methods of inquiry. As a result, his philosophy has exerted a unique influence on almost every form of knowledge in the West and it continues to be a subject of contemporary philosophical discussion.
</p><p>Little is known about his life. Aristotle was born in the city of <a href="/wiki/Stagira_(ancient_city)" title="Stagira (ancient city)">Stagira</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Greece" title="Northern Greece">Northern Greece</a>. His father, <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(father_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (father of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a>, died when Aristotle was a child, and he was brought up by a guardian. At seventeen or eighteen years of age he joined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato%27s_Academy" title="Plato's Academy">Plato's Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and remained there until the age of thirty-seven (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â347 BC</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHumphreys2009_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumphreys2009-6">[5]</a></sup> Shortly after Plato died, Aristotle left Athens and, at the request of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, tutored <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> beginning in 343 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-7">[6]</a></sup> He established a library in the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(classical)" title="Lyceum (classical)">Lyceum</a> which helped him to produce many of his hundreds of books on <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scrolls" title="Scrolls">scrolls</a>. Though Aristotle wrote many elegant treatises and dialogues for publication, only around <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Aristotelicum" title="Corpus Aristotelicum">a third of his original output has survived</a>, none of it intended for publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes19959_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes19959-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aristotle's views profoundly shaped <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval scholarship</a>. The influence of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">physical science</a> extended from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, and were not replaced systematically until <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">the Enlightenment</a> and theories such as <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> were developed. Some of Aristotle's zoological observations found in <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">his biology</a>, such as on the <a href="/wiki/Hectocotylus" title="Hectocotylus">hectocotyl (reproductive) arm</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, were disbelieved until the 19th century. He also influenced <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Islamic_philosophies_(800%E2%80%931400)" title="Judeo-Islamic philosophies (800â1400)">Judeo-Islamic philosophies</a> during the Middle Ages, as well as <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Church" title="Early Church">Early Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic</a> tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. Aristotle was revered among medieval Muslim scholars as "The First Teacher", and among medieval Christians like <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> as simply "The Philosopher", while the poet <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> called him "the master of those who know". His works contain the earliest known formal study of logic, and were studied by medieval scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Abelard" title="Peter Abelard">Peter Abelard</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Buridan" title="John Buridan">John Buridan</a>.
</p><p>Aristotle's influence on logic continued well into the 19th century. In addition, his <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_ethics" title="Aristotelian ethics">ethics</a>, though always influential, gained renewed interest with the modern advent of <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">virtue ethics</a>. Aristotle has been called the father of logic, biology, political science, zoology, embryology, natural law, scientific method, rhetoric, psychology, realism, criticism, individualism, teleology, and meteorology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LitList_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-LitList-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/220px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/330px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/440px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>School of Aristotle in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mieza,_Macedonia" title="Mieza, Macedonia">Mieza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia, Greece</a></div></div></div>
<p>In general, the details of Aristotle's life are not well-established. The biographies written in ancient times are often speculative and historians only agree on a few salient points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[B]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aristotle was born in 384Â BC in <a href="/wiki/Stagira_(ancient_city)" title="Stagira (ancient city)">Stagira</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcidice" title="Chalcidice">Chalcidice</a>, about 55Â km (34 miles) east of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLeisch19995_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLeisch19995-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristoteles-Park_in_Stagira_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristoteles-Park_in_Stagira-13">[11]</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(father_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (father of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a>, was the personal physician to <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_III_of_Macedon" title="Amyntas III of Macedon">King Amyntas of Macedon</a>. While he was young, Aristotle learned about biology and medical information, which was taught by his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> Both of Aristotle's parents died when he was about thirteen, and <a href="/wiki/Proxenus_of_Atarneus" title="Proxenus of Atarneus">Proxenus of Atarneus</a> became his guardian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall201814_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall201814-15">[13]</a></sup> Although little information about Aristotle's childhood has survived, he probably spent some time within the Macedonian palace, making his first connections with the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)#Kingship_and_the_royal_court" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian monarchy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos20134_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos20134-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the age of seventeen or eighteen, Aristotle moved to Athens to continue his education at <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Plato's Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlits199958â63_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlits199958â63-17">[15]</a></sup> He probably experienced the <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_Mysteries" title="Eleusinian Mysteries">Eleusinian Mysteries</a> as he wrote when describing the sights one viewed at the Eleusinian Mysteries, "to experience is to learn" [ÏÎ±Î¸ÎµÎ¯Î½ Î¼Î±Î¸ÎµÄ©Î½].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2006_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2006-18">[16]</a></sup> Aristotle remained in Athens for nearly twenty years before leaving in 348/47Â BC. The traditional story about his departure records that he was disappointed with the Academy's direction after control passed to Plato's nephew <a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a>, although it is possible that he feared the anti-Macedonian sentiments in Athens at that time and left before Plato died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle1984Introduction_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle1984Introduction-19">[17]</a></sup> Aristotle then accompanied <a href="/wiki/Xenocrates" title="Xenocrates">Xenocrates</a> to the court of his friend <a href="/wiki/Hermias_of_Atarneus" title="Hermias of Atarneus">Hermias of Atarneus</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. After the death of Hermias, Aristotle travelled with his pupil <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> to the island of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a>, where together they researched the <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a> and zoology of the island and its sheltered lagoon. While in Lesbos, Aristotle married <a href="/wiki/Pythias" title="Pythias">Pythias</a>, either Hermias's adoptive daughter or niece. She bore him a daughter, whom they also named Pythias. In 343 BC, Aristotle was invited by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> to become the tutor to his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShields2016_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShields2016-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2241" data-file-width="1680" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/170px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/255px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/340px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">Portrait bust</a> of Aristotle; an <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial Roman</a> (1st or 2nd century AD) copy of a lost <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">bronze sculpture</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a></div></div></div>
<p>Aristotle was appointed as the head of the royal academy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>. During Aristotle's time in the Macedonian court, he gave lessons not only to Alexander but also to two other future kings: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen199158â59_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen199158â59-21">[19]</a></sup> Aristotle encouraged Alexander toward eastern conquest, and Aristotle's own attitude towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> was unabashedly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnocentricism" title="Ethnocentricism">ethnocentric</a>. In one famous example, he counsels Alexander to be "a leader to the Greeks and a despot to the barbarians, to look after the former as after friends and relatives, and to deal with the latter as with beasts or plants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen199158â59_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen199158â59-21">[19]</a></sup> By 335Â BC, Aristotle had returned to Athens, establishing his own school there known as the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(classical)" title="Lyceum (classical)">Lyceum</a>. Aristotle conducted courses at the school for the next twelve years. While in Athens, his wife Pythias died and Aristotle became involved with <a href="/wiki/Herpyllis" title="Herpyllis">Herpyllis</a> of Stagira, who bore him a son whom he named after his father, <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(son_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (son of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a>. If the <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i> â an uncritical compilation from the Middle Ages â is accurate, he may also have had an <i><a href="/wiki/Eromenos" title="Eromenos">erÃ´menos</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Palaephatus" title="Palaephatus">Palaephatus of Abydus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200788_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200788-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>This period in Athens, between 335 and 323 BC, is when Aristotle is believed to have composed many of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-7">[6]</a></sup> He wrote many dialogues, of which only fragments have survived. Those works that have survived are in <a href="/wiki/Treatise" title="Treatise">treatise</a> form and were not, for the most part, intended for widespread publication; they are generally thought to be lecture aids for his students. His most important treatises include <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">Poetics</a></i>. Aristotle studied and made significant contributions to "logic, metaphysics, mathematics, physics, biology, botany, ethics, politics, agriculture, medicine, dance, and theatre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHumphreys2009_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumphreys2009-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Near the end of his life, Alexander and Aristotle became estranged over Alexander's relationship with Persia and Persians. A widespread tradition in antiquity suspected Aristotle of playing a role in Alexander's death, but the only evidence of this is an <a href="/wiki/Hagnothemis" title="Hagnothemis">unlikely claim</a> made some six years after the death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen1991460_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen1991460-23">[21]</a></sup> Following Alexander's death, anti-Macedonian sentiment in Athens was rekindled. In 322Â BC, Demophilus and <a href="/wiki/Eurymedon_the_Hierophant" title="Eurymedon the Hierophant">Eurymedon the Hierophant</a> reportedly denounced Aristotle for impiety,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFilonik201372â73_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFilonik201372â73-24">[22]</a></sup> prompting him to flee to his mother's family estate in <a href="/wiki/Chalcis" title="Chalcis">Chalcis, on Euboea</a>, at which occasion he was said to have stated: "I will not allow the Athenians to sin twice against philosophy"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1980216_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1980216-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGigon201741_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGigon201741-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDÃ¼ring1957T44a-e_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDÃ¼ring1957T44a-e-27">[25]</a></sup> â a reference to Athens's <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Socrates" title="Trial of Socrates">trial and execution of Socrates</a>. He died on Euboea of natural causes later that same year, having named his student <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> as his chief <a href="/wiki/Executor" title="Executor">executor</a> and leaving a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Will_(law)" title="Will (law)">will</a> in which he asked to be buried next to his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase19923862_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase19923862-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculative_philosophy">Speculative philosophy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Term_logic" title="Term logic">Term logic</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Aristotelian_logic" title="Non-Aristotelian logic">Non-Aristotelian logic</a></div>
<p>With the <i><a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a></i>, Aristotle is credited with the earliest study of formal logic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDegnan199481â89_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDegnan199481â89-29">[27]</a></sup> and his conception of it was the dominant form of Western logic until 19th-century advances in <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorcoran20091â20_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorcoran20091â20-30">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a> stated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Pure_Reason" title="Critique of Pure Reason">Critique of Pure Reason</a></i> that with Aristotle logic reached its completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKant1787Preface_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKant1787Preface-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Organon"><i>Organon</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></div>
<table class="wikitable floatright">
<caption>One of Aristotle's <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Types" title="Syllogism">types of syllogism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[C]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>In words</th>
<th>In<br/> terms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[D]</a></sup></th>
<th>In equations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[E]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Â Â Â Â All men are mortal.<br/><br/>Â Â Â Â All Greeks are men.<br/><br/><a href="/wiki/Therefore_sign" title="Therefore sign">â´</a> All Greeks are mortal.</td>
<td>M a P<br/><br/>S a M<br/><br/>S a P</td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg"><img alt="Modus Barbara Equations.svg" data-file-height="30" data-file-width="41" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/180px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/270px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/360px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>What is today called <i>Aristotelian logic</i> with its <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Types" title="Syllogism">types of syllogism</a> (methods of logical argument),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016-32">[30]</a></sup> Aristotle himself would have labelled "analytics". The term "logic" he reserved to mean <i>dialectics</i>. Most of Aristotle's work is probably not in its original form, because it was most likely edited by students and later lecturers. The logical works of Aristotle were compiled into a set of six books called the <i><a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></i> around 40 BC by <a href="/wiki/Andronicus_of_Rhodes" title="Andronicus of Rhodes">Andronicus of Rhodes</a> or others among his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPickover200952_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPickover200952-37">[32]</a></sup> The books are:
</p>
<ol><li><i><a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)">Categories</a></i></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_Interpretation" title="On Interpretation">On Interpretation</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Posterior_Analytics" title="Posterior Analytics">Posterior Analytics</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Topics_(Aristotle)" title="Topics (Aristotle)">Topics</a></i></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_Sophistical_Refutations" title="On Sophistical Refutations">On Sophistical Refutations</a></i></li></ol>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/170px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/255px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/340px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> (left) and Aristotle in <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>'s 1509 fresco, <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>. Aristotle holds his <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> and gestures to the earth, representing his view in immanent realism, whilst Plato gestures to the heavens, indicating his Theory of Forms, and holds his <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchool_of_Athens_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchool_of_Athens-38">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart2019_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart2019-39">[34]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The order of the books (or the teachings from which they are composed) is not certain, but this list was derived from analysis of Aristotle's writings. It goes from the basics, the analysis of simple terms in the <i>Categories,</i> the analysis of propositions and their elementary relations in <i>On Interpretation</i>, to the study of more complex forms, namely, syllogisms (in the <i>Analytics</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrior_Analytics24b18â20_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrior_Analytics24b18â20-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBobzien2015_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBobzien2015-41">[36]</a></sup> and dialectics (in the <i>Topics</i> and <i>Sophistical Refutations</i>). The first three treatises form the core of the logical theory <i>stricto sensu</i>: the grammar of the language of logic and the correct rules of reasoning. The <i>Rhetoric</i> is not conventionally included, but it states that it relies on the <i>Topics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics (Aristotle)</a></div>
<p>The word "metaphysics" appears to have been coined by the first century AD editor who assembled various small selections of Aristotle's works to the treatise we know by the name <i>Metaphysics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup> Aristotle called it "first philosophy", and distinguished it from mathematics and natural science (physics) as the contemplative (<i>theoretikÄ</i>) philosophy which is "theological" and studies the divine. He wrote in his <i>Metaphysics</i> (1026a16):
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> if there were no other independent things besides the composite natural ones, the study of nature would be the primary kind of knowledge; but if there is some motionless independent thing, the knowledge of this precedes it and is first philosophy, and it is universal <i>in just this way</i>, because it is first. And it belongs to this sort of philosophy to study being as being, both what it is and what belongs to it just by virtue of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle1999111_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle1999111-44">[39]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Substance">Substance</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">Hylomorphism</a></div>
<p>Aristotle examines the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a> (<i>ousia</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> (<i>to ti Ãªn einai</i>, "the what it was to be") in his <i>Metaphysics</i> (Book VII), and he concludes that a particular substance is a combination of both matter and form, a philosophical theory called <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>. In Book VIII, he distinguishes the matter of the substance as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Material_substratum" title="Material substratum">substratum</a>, or the stuff of which it is composed. For example, the matter of a house is the bricks, stones, timbers, etc., or whatever constitutes the <i>potential</i> house, while the form of the substance is the <i>actual</i> house, namely 'covering for bodies and chattels' or any other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genus-differentia_definition" title="Genus-differentia definition">differentia</a> that let us define something as a house. The formula that gives the components is the account of the matter, and the formula that gives the differentia is the account of the form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1043a_10â30_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1043a_10â30-45">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Immanent_realism">Immanent realism</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_theory_of_universals" title="Aristotle's theory of universals">Aristotle's theory of universals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/330px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/495px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/660px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">forms</a> exist as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universals" title="Universals">universals</a>, like the ideal form of an apple. For Aristotle, both matter and form belong to the individual thing (<a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>Like his teacher Plato, Aristotle's philosophy aims at the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">universal</a>. Aristotle's <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a> places the universal (<i>katholou</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Particular" title="Particular">particulars</a> (<i>kath' hekaston</i>), things in the world, whereas for Plato the universal is a separately existing form which actual things imitate. For Aristotle, "form" is still what <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a> are based on, but is "instantiated" in a particular substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato argued that all things have a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">universal form</a>, which could be either a property or a relation to other things. When one looks at an apple, for example, one sees an apple, and one can also analyse a form of an apple. In this distinction, there is a particular apple and a universal form of an apple. Moreover, one can place an apple next to a book, so that one can speak of both the book and apple as being next to each other. Plato argued that there are some universal forms that are not a part of particular things. For example, it is possible that there is no particular good in existence, but "good" is still a proper universal form. Aristotle disagreed with Plato on this point, arguing that all universals are instantiated at some period of time, and that there are no universals that are unattached to existing things. In addition, Aristotle disagreed with Plato about the location of universals. Where Plato spoke of the forms as existing separately from the things that participate in them, Aristotle maintained that universals exist within each thing on which each universal is predicated. So, according to Aristotle, the form of apple exists within each apple, rather than in the world of the forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd196843â47_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd196843â47-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Potentiality_and_actuality">Potentiality and actuality</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality_(Aristotle)" title="Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)">Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)</a></div>
<p>With regard to the change (<i><a href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality" title="Potentiality and actuality">kinesis</a></i>) and its causes now, as he defines in his <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/On_Generation_and_Corruption" title="On Generation and Corruption">On Generation and Corruption</a></i> 319bâ320a, he distinguishes the coming to be from:
</p>
<ol><li>growth and diminution, which is change in quantity;</li>
<li>locomotion, which is change in space; and</li>
<li>alteration, which is change in quality.</li></ol>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1698" data-file-width="2122" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/220px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/330px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/440px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle argued that a capability like playing the flute could be acquired â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality_(Aristotle)" title="Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)">the potential made actual</a> â by learning.</div></div></div>
<p>The coming to be is a change where nothing persists of which the resultant is a property. In that particular change he introduces the concept of potentiality (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunamis" title="Dunamis">dynamis</a></i>) and actuality (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entelecheia" title="Entelecheia">entelecheia</a></i>) in association with the matter and the form. Referring to potentiality, this is what a thing is capable of doing or being acted upon if the conditions are right and it is not prevented by something else. For example, the seed of a plant in the soil is potentially (<i>dynamei</i>) a plant, and if it is not prevented by something, it will become a plant. Potentially beings can either 'act' (<i>poiein</i>) or 'be acted upon' (<i>paschein</i>), which can be either innate or learned. For example, the eyes possess the potentiality of sight (innate â being acted upon), while the capability of playing the flute can be possessed by learning (exercise â acting). Actuality is the fulfilment of the end of the potentiality. Because the end (<i>telos</i>) is the principle of every change, and for the sake of the end exists potentiality, therefore actuality is the end. Referring then to the previous example, it can be said that an actuality is when a plant does one of the activities that plants do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> For that for the sake of which (<i>to hou heneka</i>) a thing is, is its principle, and the becoming is for the sake of the end; and the actuality is the end, and it is for the sake of this that the potentiality is acquired. For animals do not see in order that they may have sight, but they have sight that they may see.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsIX_1050a_5â10_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsIX_1050a_5â10-47">[42]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In summary, the matter used to make a house has potentiality to be a house and both the activity of building and the form of the final house are actualities, which is also a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Final_cause" title="Final cause">final cause</a> or end. Then Aristotle proceeds and concludes that the actuality is prior to potentiality in formula, in time and in substantiality. With this definition of the particular substance (i.e., matter and form), Aristotle tries to solve the problem of the unity of the beings, for example, "what is it that makes a man one"? Since, according to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> there are two Ideas: animal and biped, how then is man a unity? However, according to Aristotle, the potential being (matter) and the actual one (form) are one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-43">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1045aâb_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1045aâb-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h3>
<p>Aristotle's immanent realism means his <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> is based on the study of things that exist or happen in the world, and rises to knowledge of the universal, whereas for Plato epistemology begins with knowledge of universal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">Forms</a> (or ideas) and descends to knowledge of particular imitations of these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-42">[37]</a></sup> Aristotle uses <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a> from examples alongside <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a>, whereas Plato relies on deduction from <i><a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a></i> principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_philosophy">Natural philosophy</span></h2>
<p>Aristotle's "natural philosophy" spans a wide range of natural phenomena including those now covered by physics, biology and other natural sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-49">[44]</a></sup> In Aristotle's terminology, "natural philosophy" is a branch of philosophy examining the phenomena of the natural world, and includes fields that would be regarded today as physics, biology and other natural sciences. Aristotle's work encompassed virtually all facets of intellectual inquiry. Aristotle makes philosophy in the broad sense coextensive with reasoning, which he also would describe as "science". However, his use of the term <i>science</i> carries a different meaning than that covered by the term "scientific method". For Aristotle, "all science (<i>dianoia</i>) is either practical, poetical or theoretical" (<i>Metaphysics</i> 1025b25). His practical science includes ethics and politics; his poetical science means the study of fine arts including poetry; his theoretical science covers physics, mathematics and metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-49">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="634" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/220px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/330px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/440px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical elements</a> (fire, air, water, earth) of <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> and Aristotle illustrated with a burning log. The log releases all four elements as it is destroyed.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_elements">Five elements</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">Classical element</a></div>
<p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/On_Generation_and_Corruption" title="On Generation and Corruption">On Generation and Corruption</a></i>, Aristotle related each of the four elements proposed earlier by <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">Water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">Air</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">Fire</a>, to two of the four sensible qualities, hot, cold, wet, and dry. In the Empedoclean scheme, all matter was made of the four elements, in differing proportions. Aristotle's scheme added the heavenly <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">Aether</a>, the divine substance of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">heavenly spheres</a>, stars and planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133â139,_166â169_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133â139,_166â169-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
